[2026-03-16T15:06:07Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Q.	Persian astronomer Abd al-Rahman al-Sufi first recorded X in 964 CE in his Book of Fixed Stars, describing it as a "small cloud." Its modern name derives from the Greek mythological princess chained to a rock as a sacrifice — the constellation in which it resides. Edwin Hubble definitively resolved it into individual stars in 1923 using the 100-inch Hooker Telescope at Mount Wilson, identifying Cepheid variable stars within it that allowed him to calculate it was far beyond the bounds of our own galaxy. ID X.

[2026-03-16T15:22:02Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Q.	Messier’s Catalogue was too small, as astronomical discoveries accelerated in the 19th century. John Dreyer made the next attempt at compiling a list of objects in the night sky and assigning them specific identifying numbers. Dreyer’s 1888 work is known by the three-letter acronym X. X contains 7,840 objects, including galaxies, star clusters and emission nebulae. Dreyer published two supplements to X in 1895 and 1908, known as the Index Catalogues (abbreviated IC), describing a further 5,386 astronomical objects. Thousands of these objects are best known by their X or IC numbers, which remain in widespread use. Our galactic neighbor Andromeda gets the number 224 in X. ID X.
